In the LORD I take refuge;
how can you say to me,
“Take to the hills like a bird!-a

### INTRODUCTION ESSAY (for review)
# Introduction Essay

The ancient Israelite sage who composed Psalm 11 faced a dilemma that resonates across millennia: when violence erupts and social order crumbles, where does one find security? The psalm opens with a declaration of refuge—"בַּֽיהֹוָ֨ה ׀ חָסִ֗יתִי" (*baYHWH ḥāsîtî*, "In the LORD I have taken refuge," v. 1a)—but this confident assertion is immediately challenged by urgent counsel to flee. The poem's architecture dramatizes this tension, moving from a quoted voice of despair (vv. 1b-3) to a theological response grounded in divine sovereignty (vv. 4-7). What makes this psalm extraordinary is not that it resolves the crisis through divine intervention, but that it transforms the very terms of the question by shifting from horizontal human action to vertical divine observation.

The dialogical structure creates a vivid drama. Advisors—perhaps fellow refugees or well-meaning friends during David's flight from Saul, according to traditional interpretation—urge the psalmist: "נ֝֗וּדִי הַרְכֶ֥ם צִפּֽוֹר" (*nûdî harkhem tsippôr*, "Flee to your mountain, bird!" v. 1b). The simile is visceral: the righteous person is imagined as a vulnerable bird, hunted and exposed. The imagery intensifies with detailed archery language: "כִּ֤י הִנֵּ֪ה הָרְשָׁעִ֡ים יִדְרְכ֬וּן קֶ֗שֶׁת כּוֹנְנ֣וּ חִצָּ֣ם עַל־יֶ֑תֶר" (*kî hinnēh hārəšāʿîm yidrəḵûn qešet kônənû ḥiṣṣām ʿal-yeter*, "For behold, the wicked bend the bow, they have set their arrow on the string," v. 2). The Hebrew verbs create a sequence of menace—bending (*yidrəḵûn*), setting (*kônənû*), shooting (*lîrôt*)—that conveys the methodical preparation of an ambush. Radak notes the specific danger: "לירות במו אפל...בהסתר" (*lîrôt bəmô-ʾōfel...bəhēstēr*, "to shoot in darkness...in secret")—this is assassination, not open combat.

The counsel of despair reaches its climax in verse 3 with a rhetorical question that has puzzled interpreters for centuries: "כִּ֣י הַ֭שָּׁתוֹת יֵהָרֵס֑וּן צַ֝דִּ֗יק מַה־פָּעָֽל" (*kî haššātôt yēhārēsûn ṣaddîq mah-pāʿal*, "When the foundations are destroyed, what can the righteous do?"). What are these "foundations" (*haššātôt*)? The term appears only here in Psalms, derived from the root שׁית (*šyt*, "to set, place"). BDB suggests it could mean "foundations" or even "nets" or "snares," while Radak offers multiple readings: "השתות הם היסודות" (*haššātôt hēm hayyəsôdôt*, "the foundations are the foundations"), interpreting them as either architectural supports, cosmic foundations, or metaphorically as moral and social order. The Meiri, writing in thirteenth-century Provence, takes a historical approach, connecting this to David's experience at Keilah when the city's leaders planned to betray him to Saul. In this reading, the "foundations" are the very people—priests at Nob, citizens of Keilah—who should have been pillars of support but instead became sources of danger.

The question "צַ֝דִּ֗יק מַה־פָּעָֽל" (*ṣaddîq mah-pāʿal*) is syntactically ambiguous. The verb פָּעָֽל (*pāʿal*) is perfect tense, allowing either a retrospective reading ("what has the righteous done [to deserve this]?") or a modal one ("what can the righteous do?"). The psalm leaves this question hanging, unanswered—or rather, it answers it by changing the frame of reference entirely.

Verse 4 marks the theological pivot with a remarkable double location formula: "יְהֹוָ֤ה ׀ בְּֽהֵ֘יכַ֤ל קׇדְשׁ֗וֹ יְהֹוָה֮ בַּשָּׁמַ֢יִם כִּ֫סְא֥וֹ" (*YHWH bəhêḵal qodšô YHWH baššāmayim kisʾô*, "The LORD is in His holy temple, the LORD—His throne is in heaven"). This bicolon has generated significant interpretive debate. Does it assert simultaneous divine presence in both earthly temple and heavenly throne room, reflecting ancient Near Eastern temple theology where temples functioned as cosmic contact points? Or is "בְּֽהֵ֘יכַ֤ל קׇדְשׁ֗וֹ" (*bəhêḵal qodšô*, "His holy temple") itself a reference to the heavens, with the parallel structure simply intensifying a single claim? Ibn Ezra takes the latter view: "היכל קדשו הם השמים" (*hêḵal qodšô hēm haššāmayim*, "His holy temple is the heavens"). Either way, the effect is clear: divine sovereignty is established as the ground of response to the crisis.

What follows is one of the psalm's most striking features—the detailed anthropomorphism of divine vision. The psalm declares: "עֵינָ֥יו יֶחֱז֑וּ עַפְעַפָּ֥יו יִ֝בְחֲנ֗וּ בְּנֵ֣י אָדָֽם" (*ʿênāyw yeḥĕzû ʿapʿappāyw yivḥănû bənê ʾādām*, "His eyes behold, His eyelids test humanity," v. 4b). The specificity of "עַפְעַפָּ֥יו" (*ʿapʿappāyw*, "His eyelids") is unusual—why not simply "eyes"? The term suggests careful, deliberate scrutiny, the narrowed gaze of one examining something closely. The verb בחן (*bḥn*, "to test, examine") appears twice (vv. 4, 5), carrying metallurgical overtones of refining and testing precious metals. This creates a profound contrast with the wicked who "shoot from darkness" (v. 2). The psalm constructs an epistemological binary: the wicked operate in concealment (*bəmô-ʾōfel*, "in darkness"), assuming they are unseen, while God's comprehensive vision penetrates all pretense.

The psalm's treatment of divine testing in verse 5 has troubled interpreters. "יְהֹוָה֮ צַדִּ֢יק יִ֫בְחָ֥ן וְ֭רָשָׁע וְאֹהֵ֣ב חָמָ֑ס שָֽׂנְאָ֥ה נַפְשֽׁוֹ" (*YHWH ṣaddîq yivḥān wərāšāʿ wəʾōhēv ḥāmās śānəʾāh napšô*, "The LORD tests the righteous, but the wicked and lover of violence His soul hates"). Does God test both righteous and wicked equally, or—as the syntax might suggest—does He test the righteous while hating the wicked? Rashi famously resolves this with a parable: the flax worker beats good flax vigorously because it can withstand the treatment, but handles poor flax gently because it would disintegrate. God's testing of the righteous is thus a mark of confidence, not hostility. Metzudat David concurs: "מה שאני נרדף משאול לא הוא בעבור העון" (*mah še-ʾănî nirdāp miššāʾûl lōʾ hûʾ baʿăvôr hē-ʿāwōn*, "That I am pursued by Saul is not on account of sin")—persecution does not imply guilt.

Verse 6 unleashes a torrent of judgment imagery: "יַמְטֵ֥ר עַל־רְשָׁעִ֗ים פַּ֫חִ֥ים אֵ֣שׁ וְ֭גׇפְרִית וְר֥וּחַ זִלְעָפ֗וֹת מְנָ֣ת כּוֹסָֽם" (*yamṭēr ʿal-rəšāʿîm paḥîm ʾēš wəgop̄rît wərûaḥ zilʿāp̄ôt mənāt kôsām*, "He will rain upon the wicked snares, fire and sulfur and scorching wind—the portion of their cup"). The verb "rain" (*yamṭēr*) creates a devastating inversion: rain, normally the source of blessing in an agrarian society, becomes the vehicle of destruction. The catalog of judgment—"פַּ֫חִ֥ים" (*paḥîm*, "snares" or possibly "coals"), "אֵ֣שׁ וְ֭גׇפְרִית" (*ʾēš wəgop̄rît*, "fire and sulfur")—unmistakably evokes the destruction of Sodom and Gomorrah in Genesis 19:24: "וַיהֹוָ֗ה הִמְטִ֧יר עַל־סְדֹ֛ם וְעַל־עֲמֹרָ֖ה גָּפְרִ֣ית וָאֵ֑שׁ" (*waYHWH himṭîr ʿal-Səḏōm wəʿal-ʿĂmōrāh gop̄rît wāʾēš*, "And the LORD rained upon Sodom and Gomorrah sulfur and fire"). The allusion positions divine judgment as anti-creation, a reversal of the generative order. The phrase "מְנָ֣ת כּוֹסָֽם" (*mənāt kôsām*, "the portion of their cup") employs the metaphor of destiny as an allotted inheritance—what one receives is what one has earned.

The psalm concludes with a tripartite affirmation in verse 7: "כִּֽי־צַדִּ֣יק יְ֭הֹוָה צְדָק֣וֹת אָהֵ֑ב יָ֝שָׁ֗ר יֶחֱז֥וּ פָנֵֽימוֹ" (*kî-ṣaddîq YHWH ṣədāqôt ʾāhēv yāšār yeḥĕzû p̄ānêmô*, "For the LORD is righteous; righteousness He loves; the upright shall behold His face"). The first two cola establish divine character and divine preference—God is righteous and loves righteous deeds. But the third colon makes a startling promise: "יָ֝שָׁ֗ר יֶחֱז֥וּ פָנֵֽימוֹ" (*yāšār yeḥĕzû p̄ānêmô*, "the upright shall behold His face"). This creates a deliberate echo with verse 4's assertion that God's eyes "behold" (*yeḥĕzû*) humanity—now the direction of seeing is reversed. The upright will see God. This raises an interpretive challenge given Exodus 33:20's prohibition: "לֹ֥א תוּכַ֖ל לִרְאֹ֣ת אֶת־פָּנָ֑י כִּ֛י לֹֽא־יִרְאַ֥נִי הָאָדָ֖ם וָחָֽי" (*lōʾ ṯûḵal lirəʾōṯ ʾeṯ-pānāy kî lōʾ-yirəʾănî hāʾādām wāḥāy*, "You cannot see My face, for no human can see Me and live"). Is this cultic language, referring to encountering God in temple worship? Is it eschatological, pointing to a future beatific vision? Or is it metaphorical, describing the experience of divine favor? Radak offers multiple readings: "ופניתי אליכם" (*ûp̄ānîṯî ʾălêḵem*, "and I will turn My face toward you," Lev 26:9), suggesting divine attention and blessing. The ambiguity may be deliberate—the psalm promises not simply rescue but ultimate intimacy with the divine, however that may be conceived.

What makes Psalm 11 enduringly powerful is its refusal to answer the pragmatic question on its own terms. The advisors ask, "What can the righteous do?" The psalm does not provide a tactical manual for resistance or survival. Instead, it reframes the crisis by asserting that the ultimate reality is not the wicked's archery or the destroyed foundations, but God's comprehensive vision and discriminating justice. The psalm's movement from the horizontal plane (flee to the mountain) to the vertical axis (the LORD in heaven) represents not escapism but a reorientation of perception. The righteous do not need to *do* anything because God *sees* everything. In a world where violence operates in darkness and moral foundations seem to crumble, the psalm stakes everything on the claim that divine observation is itself a form of protection, and divine justice, however delayed, is certain. The final promise—that the upright will behold God's face—transforms refuge from a place of hiding into a position of privilege, from defensive retreat into assured encounter. This is not theodicy as explanation but theodicy as reframing, offering not answers but a different vantage point from which to view the crisis.

## Modern Jewish Liturgical Use

Psalm 11 does not appear as a complete recitation in the standard liturgical cycle, but specific phrases have found liturgical life in highly focused contexts. The phrase "עַל־רְשָׁעִ֗ים פַּ֫חִ֥ים" (*ʿal-rəšāʿîm paḥîm*, "upon the wicked snares") from verse 6 appears in a personal prayer recited in the sukkah during Sukkot, found in both Ashkenazic and Sephardic rites. This meditation, often associated with the *Ushpizin* ceremony of welcoming mystical guests, incorporates the verse into a petition that reads: "וְלַחֲסוֹת מִזֶּרֶם וּמִמָּטָר, כִּי תַמְטִיר עַל רְשָׁעִים פַּחִים" (*wəlaḥăsôṯ mizzerem ûmmimāṭār, kî ṯamṭîr ʿal rəšāʿî

### VERSE-BY-VERSE COMMENTARY (for review)
# Verse-by-Verse Commentary: Psalm 11

**Verse 1**

בַּֽיהֹוָ֨ה חָסִ֗יתִי; אֵ֭יךְ תֹּאמְר֣וּ לְנַפְשִׁ֑י: נ֝֗וּדִי הַרְכֶ֥ם צִפּֽוֹר׃

The opening colon immediately establishes the psalmist's theological stance with a forceful positional emphasis: the divine name יהוה appears first, creating an almost declarative, confrontational tone. The verb חָסִיתִי ("I have taken refuge") is a perfective form, suggesting completed action—the speaker has already made the decisive commitment of trust. The root חסה appears 37 times across Scripture, predominantly in the Psalms, where it functions as a technical term for seeking divine asylum. In Psalm 7:2, David similarly declares: יְהֹוָ֣ה אֱ֭לֹהַי בְּךָ֣ חָסִ֑יתִי ("O LORD my God, in You I have taken refuge"), positioning this vocabulary within the life-or-death urgency of political persecution. The verb may have originated in the legal-cultic practice of seeking physical sanctuary at the altar (as in 1 Kings 1:50-51, when Adonijah "seized the horns of the altar"), which would give the phrase concrete resonance: David is not speaking in abstractions but in the tangible vocabulary of asylum.

The dramatic tension erupts in the second colon with the rhetorical question: אֵיךְ תֹּאמְרוּ לְנַפְשִׁי ("How can you say to my soul?"). The interrogative אֵיךְ signals incredulity and functions as a mild rebuke. The advisors are addressing his נֶפֶשׁ—not merely his "self" in a general sense, but his vital center, the seat of his will and desire. The counsel itself—נוּדִי הַרְכֶם צִפּוֹר ("Flee to your mountain, bird!")—presents a textual crux. The Masoretic ketiv reads נודו (plural imperative), while the qere is נוּדִי (feminine singular). Radak resolves this by explaining that the ketiv addresses both body and soul (plural), while the qere focuses on the נֶפֶשׁ as the governing force of the person (feminine singular in Hebrew). The simile comparing the speaker to a vulnerable צִפּוֹר ("bird") is visceral and demeaning. The advisors are essentially saying: "You are prey." Birds in the ancient Near East were symbols of powerlessness; they flee, they hide, they are hunted. The urgency is reinforced by the imperative נוּדִי, from the root נוד, meaning "to wander" or "to flee as a fugitive"—the same root used of Cain's curse: נָע וָנָד תִּהְיֶה בָאָרֶץ ("a wanderer and a fugitive you shall be on the earth," Genesis 4:12).

The Meiri, writing in 13th-century Provence, situates this counsel in the historical context of David at Keilah. After David rescued the city from the Philistines (1 Samuel 23:1-5), its citizens planned to betray him to Saul. The Meiri reads the "mountain" as a reference to the wilderness refuges where David and his men were forced to hide. This historical grounding transforms the verse from a generic meditation on trust into a specific, urgent political crisis. The advisors' counsel is pragmatic realism: when institutions fail and social structures betray you, physical flight is the only rational response. The psalm's dramatic power derives from the collision between this urgent, reasonable counsel and the psalmist's theologically rooted refusal.

---

**Verse 2**

כִּ֤י הִנֵּ֪ה הָרְשָׁעִ֡ים יִדְרְכ֬וּן קֶ֗שֶׁת, כּוֹנְנ֣וּ חִצָּ֣ם עַל־יֶ֑תֶר, לִיר֥וֹת בְּמוֹ־אֹ֝֗פֶל לְיִשְׁרֵי־לֵֽב׃

The verse presents a vivid tableau of assassination in progress, structured as a three-stage sequence of imminent violence. The rhetoric begins with the demonstrative כִּי הִנֵּה ("For behold!"), a formula of urgent attention that appears 359 times in the Hebrew Bible, typically signaling immediate threat or theophanic presence. The attackers are identified simply as הָרְשָׁעִים ("the wicked"), a term that in the Psalms often designates not merely moral failure but active hostility toward the righteous order. The subsequent verbs create a cinematic progression: יִדְרְכוּן קֶשֶׁת ("they bend the bow"), כּוֹנְנוּ חִצָּם עַל־יֶתֶר ("they have set their arrow on the string"), לִירוֹת ("to shoot"). 

The first verb, יִדְרְכוּן, from the root דרך ("to tread, to step"), is the technical term for the physical action of bending a bow by placing one's foot on it—necessary with the powerful composite bows of antiquity. Rashi glosses this: "שֶׁכְּשֶׁהוּא חָזָק, צָרִיךְ לִיתֵּן רַגְלוֹ עָלָיו כְּשֶׁרוֹצֶה לִנְטוֹתוֹ" ("When [the bow] is strong, one must place his foot upon it when he wishes to bend it"). This technical precision creates vividness—we see the archer's stance, feel the resistance of the bow. The second verb, כּוֹנְנוּ ("they have established"), from the root כון ("to set firm, to prepare"), intensifies the threat: the arrow is not merely placed but deliberately positioned, aimed with precision. The phonetic sequence in כּוֹנְנ֣וּ חִצָּ֣ם creates assonance through the repeated ō and ṣ sounds, reinforcing the deliberateness of the action.

The purpose clause—לִירוֹת בְּמוֹ־אֹפֶל לְיִשְׁרֵי־לֵב—contains two crucial details. First, the phrase בְּמוֹ־אֹפֶל ("in darkness") uses the unusual compound preposition במו, which appears only 8 times in the Masoretic Text and seems to carry an emphatic or intensifying force. The word אֹפֶל itself denotes deep darkness or gloom—not merely night but obscurity, the hiding of treachery. Radak comments: "לירות במו אפל...בהסתר" ("to shoot in darkness...in secret"), emphasizing that this is not honorable combat but assassination. The contrast with verse 4's assertion that God's eyes "see" becomes theologically pointed: the wicked assume they operate unseen, but divine vision penetrates all concealment. This creates an epistemological binary that structures the entire psalm: human darkness versus divine illumination.

The target is specified as לְיִשְׁרֵי־לֵב ("the upright in heart"). The phrase ישרי־לב appears seven times in the Psalter (Psalms 7:11, 11:2, 32:11, 36:11, 64:11, 94:15, 97:11), always designating the morally aligned, those whose inner orientation (לֵב) is "straight" (ישר). The choice of לֵב rather than נֶפֶשׁ is significant: the heart in Hebrew anthropology is the seat of intellect and moral decision-making, not emotion. The upright are those whose fundamental moral compass is aligned with divine righteousness. The term forms an ironic counterpoint to the wicked who shoot בְּמוֹ־אֹפֶל ("in darkness")—one's path is straight (ישר), the other's is crooked and concealed. Psalm 64:4-5 presents a nearly identical scenario: "אֲשֶׁר שָׁנְנוּ כַחֶרֶב לְשׁוֹנָם, דָּרְכוּ חִצָּם דָּבָר מָר, לִירֹת בַּמִּסְתָּרִים תָּם" ("Who sharpen their tongue like a sword, they aim their arrow, a bitter word, to shoot from ambush at the blameless"). The shared vocabulary (דָּרְכוּ, חִצָּם, לִירֹת) and the parallel structure suggest these psalms draw from a common tradition of lament over political persecution and judicial corruption.

---

**Verse 3**

כִּ֣י הַ֭שָּׁתוֹת יֵהָרֵס֑וּן; צַ֝דִּ֗יק מַה־פָּעָֽל׃

This verse functions as the rhetorical climax of the advisors' argument, presenting the existential question that the psalm will ultimately refuse to answer on its own terms. The opening clause—כִּי הַשָּׁתוֹת יֵהָרֵסוּן ("For the foundations are destroyed")—contains one of the most challenging lexical cruxes in the Psalter. The word הַשָּׁתוֹת appears only here in the entire Hebrew Bible, making its meaning dependent on etymological inference and contextual guessing. It is presumably derived from the root שית ("to set, to place"), yielding the meaning "that which is set/placed," hence "foundations." BDB tentatively suggests it could mean either "foundations" (architectural or cosmic) or even "nets" or "snares," though the former is universally preferred by translators and commentators.

But what are these "foundations"? Radak offers multiple interpretive possibilities: "השתות הם היסודות...או פרושו: רשתות ומצודות" ("The foundations are the foundations...or its meaning is: nets and traps"). He eventually settles on the architectural/cosmic sense: "כי השתות יהרסון השתות הם היסודות כמו והיו שתתיה מדכאים" ("For the foundations will be destroyed—the foundations are the foundations, as in 'and its foundations shall be broken down,'" citing Isaiah 19:10). The appeal to Isaiah 19:10 (where שתותיה likely refers to the working classes or pillars of society) suggests Radak understood the term metaphorically as the foundational supports of social order. The Meiri, characteristically grounded in historical narrative, interprets these "foundations" as the very people who should have provided support but instead became sources of danger: "ויהרסו שתותיכם ר״ל יסודותיכם" ("and they will destroy your foundations, meaning your supports"). In his reading, the "foundations" were the priests of Nob (massacred by Saul in 1 Samuel 22) and the citizens of Keilah who planned betrayal—institutional pillars that collapsed under pressure.

The rhetorical question that follows—צַדִּיק מַה־פָּעָל—is syntactically ambiguous and theologically loaded. The verb פָּעַל is a perfect-tense form, which in Hebrew can function either retrospectively ("What has the righteous done [to deserve this]?") or modally ("What can the righteous do?"). The question's syntactic openness mirrors its semantic ambiguity: it could be a protest of innocence (the speaker has done nothing wrong) or an expression of helplessness (there is nothing to be done). Rashi reads it as a declaration of innocence: "דָּוִד, שֶׁלֹּא חָטָא, מַה פָּעַל בְּכָל זֹאת? אַתֶּם תִּשְׂאוּ עָוֹן, וְלֹא אֲנִי" ("David, who did not sin, what did he do in all this? You will bear the iniquity, not I"). Metzudat David similarly insists: "מה שאני נרדף משאול לא הוא בעבור העון" ("That I am pursued by Saul is not on account of sin"). But the question also functions as an implicit challenge to divine justice: if the foundations are destroyed, what recourse remains? The question is left hanging, syntactically unanswered, because the psalm's strategy is not to provide a tactical response but to change the frame of reference entirely—from horizontal (what can humans do?) to vertical (what does God see and judge?).

This verse serves as the hinge of the psalm's dialogical structure. Everything before it is the counsel of despair; everything after is the theological rebuttal. The advisors' argument reaches its logical terminus: the infrastructure of justice has collapsed (השתות יהרסון), and no amount of personal righteousness (צדיק) can alter the reality of systemic breakdown. The psalm does not refute this on empirical grounds—it does not deny that foundations are crumbling. Instead, it will pivot to a different plane of reality altogether: the cosmic courtroom where God's throne is secure and His eyes pierce all concealment.

---

**Verse 4**

יְהֹוָ֤ה בְּֽהֵיכַ֤ל קׇדְשׁ֗וֹ, יְהֹוָה֮ בַּשָּׁמַ֢יִם כִּ֫סְא֥וֹ; עֵינָ֥יו יֶחֱז֑וּ, עַפְעַפָּ֥יו יִ֝בְחֲנ֗וּ בְּנֵ֣י אָדָֽם׃

The verse marks the theological turning point of the psalm with a remarkable double-location formula that has generated significant interpretive debate. The opening bicolon—יְהֹוָה בְּהֵיכַל קׇדְשׁוֹ, יְהֹוָה בַּשָּׁמַיִם כִּסְאוֹ ("The LORD is in His holy temple; the LORD—His throne is in heaven")—juxtaposes two realms of divine presence. The repetition of the divine name יהוה (appearing here in the first and second cola, then again twice in verse 5) creates an anaphoric insistence, almost a liturgical invocation. The question is whether this bicolon asserts simultaneous divine presence in both earthly sanctuary and heavenly throne room, or whether בְּהֵיכַל קׇדְשׁוֹ is itself a reference to the heavens, with the second colon serving as an intensifying parallel. 

Ibn Ezra takes the latter view decisively: "היכל קדשו הם השמים" ("His holy temple—this is the heavens"). This reading treats the verse as synonymous parallelism, where both cola point to a single cosmic reality: God enthroned in the celestial temple. Radak, characteristically, preserves both possibilities: "היכל קדשו הם השמים...או פרושו אף על פי שבשמים כסאו עיניו יחזו" ("His holy temple is the heavens...or its meaning is: even though His throne is in heaven, His eyes behold"). The second reading allows for simultaneity—God reigns from heaven yet His presence fills the earthly sanctuary—a theology consonant with Solomon's prayer: "הִנֵּה הַשָּׁמַיִם וּשְׁמֵי הַשָּׁמַיִם לֹא יְכַלְכְּלוּךָ, אַף כִּי־הַבַּיִת הַזֶּה אֲשֶׁר בָּנִיתִי" ("Behold, heaven and the highest heaven cannot contain You, much less this house that I have built," 1 Kings 8:27). This tension between transcendence (בַּשָּׁמַיִם כִּסְאוֹ) and immanence (בְּהֵיכַל קׇדְשׁוֹ) reflects ancient Near Eastern temple theology, where temples functioned as cosmic axis points—earthly structures replicating heavenly prototypes.

The second half of the verse intensifies the focus on divine vision with striking anthropomorphism: עֵינָיו יֶחֱזוּ, עַפְעַפָּיו יִבְחֲנוּ בְּנֵי אָדָם ("His eyes behold; His eyelids test humanity"). The parallelism here moves from general to specific: first God's "eyes" (עֵינָיו) "behold" (יֶחֱזוּ), then His "eyelids" (עַפְעַפָּיו) "test" (יִבְחֲנוּ). The term עַפְעַפַּיִם (dual form, "eyelids") appears only 10 times in the Hebrew Bible, always in poetic texts, and carries connotations of careful, deliberate scrutiny. Why specify "eyelids" rather than simply "eyes"? The image suggests the narrowing of vision in concentrated examination—think of someone squinting to see detail, or a jeweler examining a stone. Job 16:16 uses the term in lament: "עַל־עַפְעַפַּי צַלְמָוֶת" ("upon my eyelids is deep darkness"), where the eyelids are the locus of seeing. Here in Psalm 11:4, the divine eyelids perform the act of בחן ("testing, examining"), a verb with metallurgical overtones—it's the process of assaying precious metals to determine their purity (cf. Jeremiah 6:27, where the prophet is appointed as a בֹּחֵן "assayer" of God's people). 

This creates a deliberate contrast with the wicked of verse 2, who "shoot from darkness" (לִירוֹת בְּמוֹ־אֹפֶל). The psalm constructs an epistemological binary: the wicked operate in concealment, assuming they are unseen; God's comprehensive vision penetrates all pretense. The verb יֶחֱזוּ will return in the psalm's conclusion (verse 7), where it is reversed: the upright יֶחֱזוּ פָנֵימוֹ ("shall behold His face"). The mutual gaze—God seeing humanity, humanity seeing God—frames the psalm's ultimate promise: divine surveillance is not merely punitive but preparatory for beatific vision. 

In Jewish liturgy, this verse's language of divine scrutiny appears in the High Holy Days liturgy, particularly in the *Unetaneh Tokef* prayer, where God is depicted as examining all creatures: "וְכָל בָּאֵי עוֹלָם יַעַבְרוּן לְפָנֶיךָ כִּבְנֵי מָרוֹן" ("All creatures pass before You like sheep")—the same searching divine gaze that tests (יִבְחֲנוּ) humanity. The placement of this imagery in the Days of Awe underscores the tradition's reading of divine observation not as passive but as discriminating judgment, the assaying of human souls against the standard of righteousness.

---

**Verse 5**

יְהֹוָה֮ צַדִּ֢יק יִ֫בְחָ֥ן, וְ֭רָשָׁע וְאֹהֵ֣ב חָמָ֑ס שָֽׂנְאָ֥ה נַפְשֽׁוֹ׃

This verse presents a theological assertion followed by an emotional intensity that has troubled interpreters. The opening clause—יְהֹוָה צַדִּיק יִבְחָן ("The LORD tests the righteous")—picks up the verb בחן from the previous verse, but now explicitly identifies the צַדִּיק as the object of divine testing. The syntax permits two readings: either "The LORD, being righteous, tests [both righteous and wicked]" or "The LORD tests the righteous [but the wicked...]"—the latter creating an antithesis. Radak opts for the first reading, explaining that God's testing of the righteous is actually a mark of divine favor, not hostility: "על דרך כי את אשר יאהב יי' יוכיח" ("according to the manner of 'for whom the LORD loves, He reproves,'" citing Proverbs 3:12). This theme—that divine testing refines rather than punishes—becomes central to the verse's reception history.

Rashi famously explicates this through a parable that has become paradigmatic in Jewish interpretation: "הַפִּשְׁתָּנִי הַזֶּה כָּל זְמַן שֶׁיּוֹדֵעַ שֶׁפִּשְׁתָּנוֹ יָפָה הוּא מַקִּישׁ עָלֶיהָ, וּכְשֶׁאֵינוֹ יָפָה הוּא מְמַעֵט בִּכְתִישָׁה" ("The flax worker—as long as he knows his flax is of high quality, he beats it vigorously; but when it is not of high quality, he reduces the beating"). The parable turns the verse from a theodicy problem into a theology of confidence: God's testing is diagnostic of the righteous person's spiritual resilience, not a sign of divine displeasure. Metzudat David echoes this: "מה שאני נרדף משאול לא הוא בעבור העון רק כן דרך ה' להבחין את הצדיק ביסורים" ("That I am pursued by Saul is not because of sin; rather, it is the LORD's way to test the righteous through afflictions"). The verb בחן thus functions as a technical term for refinement-through-trial, positioning suffering as pedagogical rather than retributive.

The second half of the verse introduces an antithesis with startling emotional force: וְרָשָׁע וְאֹהֵב חָמָס שָׂנְאָה נַפְשׁוֹ ("but the wicked and the lover of violence—His soul hates"). The compound object—רָשָׁע וְאֹהֵב חָמָס—uses hendiadys to intensify the description: not merely "the wicked" but "the wicked, specifically those who love violence." The term חָמָס (violence, wrongdoing, injustice) appears 60 times in the Hebrew Bible, predominantly in contexts of social oppression and judicial corruption. In Genesis 6:11, it describes the primordial violence that provoked the Flood: "וַתִּמָּלֵא הָאָרֶץ חָמָס" ("and the earth was filled with violence"). In Habakkuk 1:2-3, the prophet laments: "לָמָּה תַרְאֵנִי אָוֶן וְעָמָל תַּבִּיט, וְשֹׁד וְחָמָס לְנֶגְדִּי" ("Why do You make me see iniquity and look at trouble? Destruction and violence are before me"). Here in Psalm 11:5, the construct אֹהֵב חָמָס ("lover of violence") suggests not episodic wrongdoing but a dispositional commitment to injustice—violence as a cultivated preference.

The response to such persons is described with unusual anthropomorphism: שָׂנְאָה נַפְשׁוֹ ("His soul hates"). The verb שָׂנֵא ("to hate") is the antonym of אָהֵב ("to love"), creating a semantic chiasm: the wicked *love* violence; God's soul *hates* them. But the attribution of "soul" (נֶפֶשׁ) to God is striking—נֶפֶשׁ in Hebrew anthropology is the vital principle, the seat of desire and emotion. Its use here intensifies the divine response beyond judicial disapproval to something more visceral and personal. Leviticus 26:30 uses similar language: "וְגָעֲלָה נַפְשִׁי אֶתְכֶם" ("and My soul shall abhor you"), where divine נֶפֶשׁ expresses profound revulsion. The effect is to position divine hatred not as arbitrary wrath but as an essential attribute of a morally ordered cosmos—God's very being (נֶפֶשׁ) is incompatible with the lover of violence. The verse thus moves from the testing of the righteous (a refining process) to the hatred of the violent (an ontological incompatibility), setting up the judgment imagery that will explode in verse 6.

---

**Verse 6**

יַמְטֵ֥ר עַל־רְשָׁעִ֗ים פַּ֫חִ֥ים, אֵ֣שׁ וְ֭גׇפְרִית וְר֥וּחַ זִלְעָפ֗וֹת מְנָ֣ת כּוֹסָֽם׃

This verse unleashes a torrent of judgment imagery, dense with allusion and theological freight. The opening verb—יַמְטֵר ("He will rain")—immediately invokes the divine action against Sodom and Gomorrah: "וַיהֹוָה הִמְטִיר עַל־סְדֹם וְעַל־עֲמֹרָה גָּפְרִית וָאֵשׁ מֵאֵת יְהֹוָה מִן־הַשָּׁמָיִם" ("And the LORD rained upon Sodom and Gomorrah sulfur and fire from the LORD out of heaven," Genesis 19:24). The verb מטר normally appears in contexts of blessing—rain as the source of agricultural fertility in a drought-prone land. Its transformation into an instrument of destruction creates a devastating inversion: what should give life becomes the vehicle of death. The psalmist thus positions divine judgment as anti-creation, a reversal of the generative cosmic order.

The catalog of judgment elements is both specific and cumulative: פַּחִים, אֵשׁ וְגׇפְרִית, וְרוּחַ זִלְעָפוֹת. The first term, פַּחִים, is lexically ambiguous. BDB tentatively suggests two possibilities: "snares" (traps for birds) or "coals" (burning embers). The LXX reads παγίδας ("snares"), and most ancient versions follow suit. But Rashi, followed by many medieval commentators, interprets it as פֶּחָמִים ("coals, charcoal"): "פחים לשון פחמים" ("pahim—an expression of coals"). Ibn Ezra offers a mediating position, connecting it to the phrase רִקֻּעֵי פַחִים from Numbers 17:3 (hammered metal plates from the censers of Korah's rebels): "רקועי פחים והם כדמות אבנים שורפים" ("hammered plates—they are like burning stones"). Regardless of the precise lexical referent, the term clearly contributes to the verse's imagery of inescapable destruction.

The phrase אֵשׁ וְגׇפְרִית ("fire and sulfur/brimstone") is the unmistakable echo of the Sodom tradition. The word גָּפְרִית appears only 7 times in the Hebrew Bible, always in contexts of total annihilation: beyond Genesis 19:24, it appears in Deuteronomy 29:22 (describing the land's desolation after covenant curse), Job 18:15 (the fate of the wicked), Isaiah 30:33 and 34:9 (theophanic judgment), and Ezekiel 38:22 (eschatological warfare against Gog). The consistent association with fire suggests a specific material reality—elemental sulfur burns with intense heat and produces choking fumes—that becomes a theological symbol for divine retribution. The allusion to Sodom is not merely illustrative but typological: the wicked of the present are aligned with the archetypal perpetrators of violence and injustice, and their fate will mirror that primordial judgment.

The third element—רוּחַ זִלְעָפוֹת—is lexically challenging. The noun זִלְעָפוֹת appears only twice elsewhere: Lamentations 5:10 ("מִפְּנֵי זַלְעֲפוֹת רָעָב," "because of the scorching heat of famine") and Psalm 119:53 ("זִלְעָפָה אֲחָזַתְנִי מֵרְשָׁעִים," "hot indignation seizes me because of the wicked"). BDB suggests it derives from a root meaning "to burn, to be hot," yielding "scorching wind" or "burning rage." Radak notes: "זלעפות הזי"ן בחיר"ק והעי"ן בקמ"ץ מן זלעפה אחזתני: ענין רעדה, או ענין שרפה" ("zilʿafot—the zayin with a ḥireq and the ʿayin with a qamatz, from 'hot indignation seizes me': an expression of trembling or an expression of burning"). The term thus combines physical heat with emotional intensity—a "scorching wind" that is simultaneously meteorological and psychological, external and internal. In the ancient Near East, the sirocco (hot desert wind) was a recognized natural disaster, capable of desiccating crops and causing respiratory distress. Here it becomes another instrument in the divine arsenal of anti-blessing.

The final phrase—מְנָת כּוֹסָם ("the portion of their cup")—employs the widespread biblical metaphor of destiny as an allotted inheritance. The term מָנָה ("portion") appears 14 times in the Hebrew Bible, often in contexts of dividing land or distributing food. In Psalm 16:5, the speaker declares: "יְהֹוָה מְנָת־חֶלְקִי וְכוֹסִי" ("The LORD is my allotted portion and my cup"). The metaphor of the "cup" (כּוֹס) as one's divinely assigned fate is especially prominent in judgment oracles. Jeremiah 25:15-28 presents an extended vision of the "cup of the wine of wrath" (כּוֹס יֵין הַחֵמָה) that all nations must drink. Isaiah 51:17, 22 similarly describes Jerusalem drinking "the cup of His fury" (כּוֹס חֲמָתוֹ). Psalm 75:9 declares: "כִּי כוֹס בְּיַד־יְהֹוָה וְיַיִן חָמַר מָלֵא מֶסֶךְ...אַךְ־שְׁמָרֶיהָ יִמְצוּ יִשְׁתּוּ כֹּל רִשְׁעֵי־אָרֶץ" ("For there is a cup in the LORD's hand, with foaming wine fully mixed...all the wicked of the earth shall drink it down to the dregs"). The use of כּוֹס as a metaphor for judgment thus links Psalm 11:6 to a rich tradition of prophetic oracles depicting divine retribution as an intoxicating, inescapable drink.

---

**Verse 7**

כִּֽי־צַדִּ֣יק יְ֭הֹוָה, צְדָק֣וֹת אָהֵ֑ב; יָ֝שָׁ֗ר יֶחֱז֥וּ פָנֵֽימוֹ׃

The psalm concludes with a tripartite affirmation that moves from divine character through divine preference to eschatological promise. The opening clause—כִּי־צַדִּיק יְהֹוָה ("For the LORD is righteous")—provides the theological ground (כִּי) for all that has preceded. The term צַדִּיק, when applied to God, functions not merely as a moral attribute but as an ontological claim: righteousness is not something God *has* but something God *is*. This is covenant theology's bedrock—divine צֶדֶק (righteousness) guarantees the moral order of the cosmos and the ultimate vindication of the oppressed. Deuteronomy 32:4 declares: "הַצּוּר תָּמִים פָּעֳלוֹ, כִּי כָל־דְּרָכָיו מִשְׁפָּט; אֵל אֱמוּנָה וְאֵין עָוֶל, צַדִּיק וְיָשָׁר הוּא" ("The Rock, His work is perfect, for all His ways are justice; a God of faithfulness and without iniquity, righteous and upright is He"). The adjective צַדִּיק, when used of God, thus anchors Israel's theological confidence: if God is fundamentally righteous, then the apparent triumph of the wicked cannot be the final word.

The second colon—צְדָקוֹת אָהֵב—intensifies the claim with an unusual plural form. The noun צְדָקָה appears in the singular 157 times in the Hebrew Bible, but here the plural צְדָקוֹת raises interpretive questions. Is this an abstract plural of intensification ("great righteousness") or a concrete plural referring to specific "righteous acts" or "deeds of righteousness"? BDB suggests both are possible. Radak interprets it as a construct phrase with an implicit subject: "צדקות איש צדקות" ("righteous acts—a man of righteous acts"), treating צְדָקוֹת as analogous to the construction in Psalm 109:4, "וַאֲנִי תְפִלָּה" ("but I am prayer," meaning "a man of prayer"). If צְדָקוֹת is concrete, then the verse asserts that God loves not just the abstract quality of righteousness but the specific, embodied righteous deeds performed by human agents. This reading has profound ethical implications: divine favor is directed toward human moral action in history, not merely toward an abstract principle. The verb אָהֵב ("He loves") is active and volitional—God's relationship to righteousness is not dispassionate judicial observation but passionate affirmation.

The final colon—יָשָׁר יֶחֱזוּ פָנֵימוֹ—contains both a grammatical puzzle and a theological promise. The word יָשָׁר ("upright") can function either as an adjective serving as a collective noun ("the upright") or as an abstract nominal ("uprightness"). The verb יֶחֱזוּ ("they shall behold") is plural, suggesting the first reading: "the upright shall behold." This creates a deliberate echo with verse 4, where God's eyes "behold" (עֵינָיו יֶחֱזוּ) humanity. The direction of seeing is now reversed: divine observation in verse 4 leads to human vision in verse 7. But what does it mean to "behold His face" (פָנֵימוֹ)? The phrase creates immediate tension with the prohibition in Exodus 33:20: "לֹא תוּכַל לִרְאֹת אֶת־פָּנָי, כִּי לֹא־יִרְאַנִי הָאָדָם וָחָי" ("You cannot see My face, for no human can see Me and live").

Medieval commentators offer multiple resolutions. Ibn Ezra proposes that פָנֵימוֹ is used metaphorically for divine favor: "פני ה' בעושי רע" ("the face of the LORD is against evildoers," Psalm 34:17), where "face" signifies attention and disposition, not literal visual perception. Radak suggests the phrase parallels Leviticus 26:9, "וּפָנִיתִי אֲלֵיכֶם" ("and I will turn My face toward you"), which describes divine blessing and covenant faithfulness rather than physical appearance. He also allows for an eschatological reading: "או פרוש ישר יחזו פנימו: על דרך אני בצדק אחזה פניך" ("or the meaning of 'the upright shall behold His face' is according to 'I shall behold Your face in righteousness,'" citing Psalm 17:15)—a vision reserved for the righteous in the world to come. The Malbim, writing in the 19th century with full awareness of modern philosophical challenges, argues that the phrase refers to the experience of divine favor and intimacy achieved through ethical striving: "ישר יחזו פנימו אם הצדקות יביטו ישר נוכח פני ה', היינו אם מטרת הצדקות וכוונתם הוא לשם ה' בלבד" ("the upright shall behold His face—if the righteous acts look straight toward the face of the LORD, that is, if the goal and intention of the righteous acts is for the sake of the LORD alone").

The ambiguity of the phrase—cultic (seeing God in temple worship), eschatological (future beatific vision), or metaphorical (experiencing divine favor)—may be deliberate. The psalm's trajectory has moved from horizontal flight (verse 1) to vertical divine presence (verse 4) to judgment (verse 6) to ultimate intimacy (verse 7). The final promise transforms refuge (חָסִיתִי, verse 1) from a defensive posture into an achieved relationship. The upright do not merely survive; they *see*. This vision is the telos of trust, the end toward which the entire psalm moves: not merely the defeat of the wicked but the beatific encounter with the righteous God whose "soul hates violence" and whose delight is in righteousness. The psalm thus offers not tactical advice for surviving persecution but a theological reframing: the question is not "what can the righteous do?" but "what will the righteous see?" The answer: the face of the One who has seen them all along.

### בְּמוֹ־אֹפֶל
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: בְּ  

prep. in (Moab. בּ, Syriac ܒ, Arabic ب, Ethiopic በ ) before tone-syllables in certain cases (Ges§ 102. 2) בָּ, with suff. בִּי; בְּךָ (Ex 7:29 Kt. 2 S 22:30 ψ 141:8 בְּכָה), in pause and fem. בָּךְ; בּוֹ (once, Je 17:24 Kt בֹּה), בָּהּ; בָּנוּ; בָּכֶם, [בָּכֶן]; בָּהֶם, בָּם [also בָּהֵ֫מָּה †Ex 30:4; 36:1 Hb 1:16], בָּהֶן †1 S 31:7 Is 38:16 Ez 42:14 [15 times בָּהֵן (FrMM 235), and thrice, Lv 5:22 Nu 13:19 Je 5:17, בָּהֵ֫נָּה]. Prep. denoting properly in, Gk. ἐν, but applied in many derived and fig. significations. The senses expressed by בְּ are grouped by the Rabbis in three classes, בֵּית הַכְּלִי Beth vasculi, בֵּית הַדִּיבִּוּק וְהַנְּגִיעָה Beth coniunctionis et viciniae, בֵּית הָעֵזֶר Beth auxilii; and the same arrangement may be followed here, though the limits between the three classes are not clearly defined, and they sometimes overlap one another. In: strictly, of position in a place (which often is expressed more precisely by בְּקֶרֶב, בְּתוֹךְ) as בַּבַּיִת in the house, בָּעִיר in the city, בַּדּוּד in the pot, בָּאָרֶץ in the land, etc. constantly. Heb. idiom also says בָּהָר in the mountain Ex 24:18 etc., even in cases where we could hardly avoid saying on, as 1 K 11:7; 19:11: so בְּחֹרֵב Dt 1:6 al.; בְּרֹאשׁ הָהָר Ex 24:17 ψ 72:16. Preceded by a verb of motion (especially עָבַר) בְּ = through, as Gn 12:6 and Abram passed through בָּאָרֶץ in the land = passed through it, 13:17 2 S 24:2 +; in (= through) a gate, Is 62:10 Je 17:19 Mi 2:13. Fig. to speak בְּאָזְנֵי in the ears of …; to be good (or evil, etc.) בְּעֵינֵי in the eyes of … of presence in the midst of a multitude, among Ex 14:28 there was not left בָּהֶם among them even one, Lv 26:36 2 S 15:31 Ahitophel בַּקּשְׁרִים is among the conspirators, 2 K 18:5 בְּכֹל מַלְכֵי יְהוּדָה. So בְּךָ in thee (of Israel, coll.) Dt 7:14; 15:4, 7; 18:10; 23:11; 28:54 (diff. from עִמָּ֑ךְ beside thee Lv 25:35).—Spec. of an individ., implying eminency among: Jos 14:15 Je 46:18 Tabor among the mountains, 49:15 Pr 30:30 Ct 1:8 הַיָּפָה בַנָּשִׁים the fair one (= the fairest) among women, La 1:1: cf. Luke 1:42. On 1 S 17:12 v. Dr. hence with some verbs, when the action refers to only a part of the object, as הִכָּהַ בְּ to smite among … i.e. to smite some of … 2 S 2:31; cf. הָפַךְ בְּ Am 4:11; הָרַג בְּ ψ 78:31; נָשָׂא בְּ to bear in, i.e. to share in bearing, Nu 11:17 al.; בָּנָה בְּ to build in or at Zc 6:15 Ne 4:4; עָשָׂה בְּ Ex 5:9; עָמַל בְּ to toil on Jon 4:10 ψ 127:1; אָכַל בְּ, שָׁתָה בְּ to eat or drink of Ju 13:16 Pr 9:5; חָלַק בְּ to give a share in Jb 39:17. specifying the parts of which a whole consists (especially in P) Gn 7:21; 8:17; 9:10, 16; 17:23 Ex 12:19 Nu 31:11, 26 Ho 4:3. with ref. to the limits enclosing a space, within: Ex 20:10 בִּשְׁעָרֶיךָ within thy gates, Is 56:5 בְּחוֹמֹתַי within my walls. often pregn. with verbs of motion, when the movement to a place results in rest in it, into: after בָּא Gn 19:8 Is 19:23; נָתַן Gn 27:17; שָׁלַח to send Lv 16:22; in among Jos 23:7, 12 1 K 11:2.—Ho 12:7 (an extreme case) שׁוּב בְּ to return (and rest) in thy God, 1 S 16:3 (unless לַזָּבַ֑ח should be read, as v 5).—עַיִן בְּעַיִן (with) eye (looking) into eye †Nu 14:14 Is 52:8; פָּנִים בְּפָנִים †Dt 5:4; עֵינֵי פְ׳ בְּ the eyes of … are upon, both in favourable (Dt 11:12 ψ 101:6) and hostile (Am 9:8 Jb 7:8) sense. applied to time, as Gn 1:1 בְּרֵאשִׁית in the beginning; 2:2 בַּיּוֹם הַשְּׁבִיעִי on the seventh day (v. יוֹם 7 g); Ju 10:8 בַּשָּׁנָה הַהִיא in that year; & constantly. of a state or condition, whether material or mental, in which an action takes place: so בְּשָׁלוֹם in peace Gn 15:15; בַּצָּרָה ψ 81:8 בְּצָ׳ 91:15 in distress; הָלַךְ בְּתֻמּוֹ to walk in his integrity Pr 28:6; Ex 5:19 בְּרָע in evil case. בְּ introduces the predicate, denoting it as that in which the subj. consists, or in which it shews itself (the Beth essentiae,—common in Arabic, especially with a ptcp. or adj. and in a negative sentence: Qor 2:79 وَمَا آللَّهُ بِغَافِلٍ and God (appears) not as one remiss; 2:7 وَمَا هُمْ بِمُؤْمِنِينَ and they are not believers [comp. French en—en honnête homme]; v. WAG ii. § 56 a): viz. a primary pred., Ex 18:4 the God of my fathers בְּעֶזְרִי was my help, ψ 146:5 Ho 13:9 (read כִּי מִי בְעֶזְרֶֽךָ ׃ with 𝔊 𝔖 Che al.); ψ 68:5 בְּיָהּ שְׁמוֹ his name consists in Yah, Jb 23:13; 37:10. With the pred. in the pl. (as pl. maj.) ψ 118:7 י׳ בְּעֹזְרָ֑י J. is my great helper, 54:5 (v. Che), Ju 11:35. a secondary pred., Ex 6:3 and I appeared unto them בְּאֵל שַׁדָּ֑י as God Almighty, Nu 26:53 בְּנַחֲלָה as an inheritance, 34:2; Ez 46:16: 47:14 Dt 10:22; 26:14 I have not put away therefrom בְּטָמֵא as one unclean = while unclean, 28:62 Is 40:10 בְּחָזָק יָבוֹא he cometh as a strong one, ψ 35:2 (where v. De) and rise up as my help, v 16, 39:7; 55:19; Pr 3:26; Jb 36:32 (De); cf. Dt 33:26; 1 S 17:11; Na 3:9. a pred. as accus., Dt 1:13 וַאֲשִׂימֵם בְּרָאשֵׁיכֶם that I may make them heads over you; Jos 21:12; Ez 20:41; Nu 18:26; 36:2; Jos 13:6, 7; 23:4; Ez 45:1; 47:22(×2) (read יַפִּלוּ) 48:29 (v. 𝔊 Co) all בְּנַחֲלָה (cf. b.), ψ 78:55 & allotted it בְּחֶבֶל נַחֲלָה as a line of inheritance (i.e. as a measured inheritance). Is 26:4 כִּי בְּיָהּ יהוה צוּר עוֹלָמִים י׳ is different—for in Yah there is a rock of ages (cf. Qor 4:7, 47 there is sufficiency in God as a patron). in comparisons, ψ 37:20 consume away בֶּעָשָׁן in the form of, as, smoke 78:33; 102:4; Jb 34:36; 36:14. Denoting proximity— at, by (rare): Dt 2:4 1 S 29:1 בָּעַיִן by the spring; Ez 10:15, 20 בִּנְהַר כְּבָר by the river Chebar (1:3 עַל) ψ 27:6. on: Gn 8:20 Nu 23:2 בַּמִּזְבֵּחַ on the altar; Ju 8:21 on the necks of the camels; 1 K 2:5 al. בְּמָתְנָיו on his loins; Is 59:17 a helmet בְּרֹאשׁוֹ on his head. often with verbs of touching, approaching, taking hold of, cleaving, etc., as אָחַז, דָּבֵק, חָשַׁק, נָגַע, נָגַשׁ, קָרַב, פָּגַּע, תָּמַךְ, תָּפַשׂ (see these words). with words expressing or implying an act of hostility— against: Gn 16:12 יָדוֹ בַכֹּל וְיַד כֹּל בּוֹ his hand against all, and the hand of all against him; הָֽיְתָה יַד פְּ׳ בּ Dt 13:10; 1 S 5:9; 18:17 + often Hence after verbs (q.v.) of fighting (נִלְחַם, רִיב), going up to invade (עָלָה), being angry (הִתְעַבֵּר, אָנַף, חָרָה אַף), sinning or acting treacherously (חָטָא, בָּגַד, מָרַד, מָרָה, מָעַל, פָּשַׁע, שִׁקֵּר, כִּזֵּב, כִּחֵשׁ), testifying (עָנָה, הֵעִיד), mocking (הִלְעִיג, הֵתֵל), feeling loathing (קוּץ), rebuking (גָּעַר: proposes to protest loudly), speaking (דִּבֶּר: Nu 12:8b), etc., & even (an extreme case) Ho 7:14 יָסוּרוּ בִי they turn aside (so as to be) against me. In a weaker sense מַשְׁתִּין בַּקִּיר mingens ad parietem 1 S 25:22 al. down to, upon (super with accus.), especially in such phrases as דָּמָיו בּוֹ Lv 20:9 al. his blood be upon him; דָּמוֹ בְרֹאשׁוֹ his blood be upon his head Jos 2:19; שׁוּב (הֵשִׁיב י׳) בְּרֹאשׁוֹ Ju 8:57 (רָעָה), 1 K 2:33 (דָּם), v 44 (רָעָה), ψ 7:17 (עֲמָלוֹ); נָתַן דֶּרֶךְ פּ׳ בְּרֹאשׁוֹ 1 K 8:32 & often in Ez, as 9:10; 11:21. With— of accompaniment: Nu 20:20 בְּעַם כָּבֵד with much people, Jos 22:8; Ju 11:34; 1 K 10:2; 2 K 5:9; Je 41:15; Ex 21:22 and he shall give בִּפְלִלִים with arbitrators (arbitrators being employed), Is 8:16 בְּלִמֻּדָֽי׃ with my disciples, i.e. in their presence; Ex 8:1, 13 Je 11:19 עֵץ בְּלַחְמוֹ a tree with its sap (s. לֵחַ); 1 K 19:19 וְהוּא בִּשְׁנֵים הֶעָשָׂר and he with the 12th. often of what one takes or brings with one: Gn 32:11 בְּמַקְלִי with my staff I passed over Jordan, Ju 11:34; 15:1; 1 S 1:24; Is 7:24; Mi 6:6 al. קִדֵּם בְּ to go to meet with; בָּא בְּ to come with Lv 16:3 ψ 66:13; 71:16. (In Arabic this usage is developed more fully than in Hebrew, and أَتَى بِ lit. to come with, ذَهَبَ بِ lit. to go away with, are used idiomatically in the sense of to bring, and to take away respectively: WAG ii. § 56 b.) Hence בְּאֵין, בְּאֶפֶס, בִּבְלִי, בְּלֹא = without. of concomitant (or surrounding) conditions, as בְּחִפָּזוֹן with (or in) haste; בְּצֶדֶק with (or in) righteousness; בִּשְׁגָגָה in error; בִּתְרוּעָה וּבְקוֹל שׁוֹפָר 2 S 6:15; often in such phrases as בְּצִדְקָֽתְךָ ψ 31:2 in thy righteousness; בְּחַסְדְּךָ in thy mercy Ex 15:13;—בְּאָשְׁרִי with my happiness! = happy am I Gn 30:13; ψ 29:4 the voice of י׳ is בַּכֹּחַ with power = is powerful; Ex 32:12 בְּרָעָה with evil purpose; ψ 73:8 בְרָע in wickedness; 90:10 בִּגְבוּרֹת with strength. of the instrument or means: as בַּחֶרֶב with the sword Ex 5:3 etc.; בְּרַגְלַיִם with the feet Is 28:3; בְּיַד … by the hand of … (v. sub יָד); to stone בְּאֶבֶן or בָּֽאֲבָנִים with stones Lv 20:2; Nu 14:10 etc.; שָׁתָה בְּ to drink with a cup Gn 44:5; Am 6:6 (cf. in Aramaic Dn 5:2); to cry בְּגָרוֹן with the throat Is 58:1; to burn בָּאֵשׁ in or with fire (often); to slay or to perish בָּרָעָב through hunger or בַּדֶּבֶר through pestilence Ex 16:3; Je 21:9 + often (cf. Jb 27:15 בַּמָּוֶת יִקָּבֵ֑רוּ); to save with or by Ju 7:7; 1 S 14:6. idiom., with certain verbs, as שָׁחַק בְּ to play with Jb 40:29; עָבַד בְּ to labour with a person (as with an instrument), i.e. to use him as a slave Ex 1:14; Je 22:13; 27:7 al.; עָשָׂה בְּ Je 18:23; Ne 9:24; Dn 11:7; Est 1:15; 2:11; 3:11; 6:6; & perhaps דִּבֶּר בְּ (of God) to speak with one Nu 12:2, 8a al. (v. Ew§ 217 f. (3); DrSm ii. 23, 2). Further בַּבַּעַל, הִתְנַבֵּא ביהוה to prophesy with or by י׳ or Baal, י׳ or Baal being the inspirer; שָׁאַל בְּ, דָּרַשׁ to inquire or ask by means of a god (or oracle). ביהוה through י׳ (= by His aid) in many connexions, as ψ 18:30; 44:6; 56:5, 11; 60:14 Is 26:13; 45:25 Ho 1:7 Zc 10:12; with pass. verbs Dt 33:29 Is 45:17 (to be saved): and even of the immediate cause Nu 36:2 to be commanded ביהוה by י׳, Gn 9:6 בָּאָדָם by man shall his blood be shed; cf. Ho 6:5; 12:14; 14:4 Jb 27:15. allied is the use of בְּ in such phrases as to bless, swear, speak, prophesy, etc., בְּשֵׁם in the name of … (i.e. the name being used or appealed to in the act) Dt 6:13 Je 11:21, etc. (so to swear ביהוה by י׳ Jos 2:12 etc., בִּימִינוֹ Is 62:8; to bless בְּךָ with thee Gn 48:20, to swear with me ψ 102:9, i.e. using my name in oath, Je 29:22); almost = in the authority and power of 1 S 17:45; 25:5; 1 K 21:8; Mi 4:5; Zc 10:12; ψ 20:6; 44:6; 89:25. בַּמָּה or בַּמֶּה by means of what? how? Gn 15:8; Ju 6:15; 16:5, 6, 10; 1 K 22:21; Mal 2:17. בְּזֹאת by means of this Gn 42:15, 33; Ex 7:17; Nu 16:28; with this = on this condition Gn 34:15, 22 1 S 11:2; Is 27:9. of cost or price (the Beth pretii), the price, whether given or received, being treated as the instrumental means by which the act is accomplished, with, for, at the cost of: thus regularly with שָׂכַר hire Gn 30:16, פָּדָה redeem Ex 34:20, אֵרֵשׂ betroth 2 S 3:14, קָנָה buy 2 S 24:24; 1 K 2:23 בְּנַפְשׁוֹ at the cost of his life hath A. spoken this word, 2 S 23:17 who went בְּנַפְשׁתָם at peril of their lives, Pr 7:23; La 5:9; Jos 6:26 בִּבְכֹרוֹ יְיַסְּדֶנָּה at the price of his firstborn shall he lay its foundations, 1 Ch 12:19 בְּרָאֹשֵׁינוּ to the jeopardy of our heads he will fall away, etc. with מָכַר sell Dt 21:14; עָבַד serve Gn 29:18, 20; Ho 12:13; הֵמִיר exchange Lv 27:10; Ho 4:7 their glory I will exchange for ignominy, ψ 106:20; נָתַן בְּנֶשֶׁךְ to give for interest Lv 25:37; ψ 15:5; in other connexions Gn 23:9; 47:16 f.; Is 45:13; La 1:11; Ct 8:7, 11; Dt 19:21 נֶפֶשׁ בְּנֶפֶשׁ עַיִן בְּעַיִן life for life, eye for eye; Is 2:22 בַּמֶּה נֶחְשָׁב הוּא at what is he to be accounted? 7:23 a thousand vines בְּאֶלֶף כָּ֑סֶף at a thousand (shekels of) silver.—Hence (perhaps) the idiom. usages שָׁנָה בְשָׁנָה year for year, one year like another, yearly Dt 15:20 1 S 1:7 +; יוֹם בְּיוֹם (late), כְּיוֹם בְּיוֹם †1 S 18:10; כְּפַעַם בְּפַעַם (v. יוֹם, פַּעַם); חֹדֶשׁ בְּהֹדֶשׁ †1 Ch 27:1. rather peculiarly, in certain cases where the object of an action may be treated as the instrument by which it is effected: as הֵנִיעַ בְּרֹאֹשׁ to shake with the head ψ 109:25 Jb 16:4 (הֵנִיעַ רֹאֹשׁ ψ 22:8 La 2:15 Is 37:22); to open with the mouth Jb 16:10, with the lips ψ 22:8; to gnash with the teeth Jb 16:9 (to gnash the teeth ψ 35:16); to wink with the eye Pr 6:13 (to wink the eye, ib. 10:10); נָתַן בְּקוֹל to utter with the voice ψ 46:7; 68:34 Je 12:8 (but נָתַן קוֹל is more common); to stretch out with the hand La 1:17; הֵרִים בַּמַּטֶּה (unusual) Ex 7:20. So קָרָא בְּשֵׁם to call with the name—in diff. senses, according to the context, viz. to proclaim Ex 33:19; 34:5; Is 44:5; ψ 49:12; to invoke Gn 4:26; 12:8; 1 K 18:24–26; Is 12:4; to name honourably Is 43:1; 45:3. Cf. Ew§ 282 d Ges§ 119. 3 b R. WAG ii. § 56 b β De Jb 31:12; Pr 20:30. with a causal force, through, on account of: Gn 18:28 הֲתַשְׁחִית בַּחֲמִשָּׁה wilt thou destroy on account of five the whole city? Lv 26:39 Nu 16:26; Dt 9:4, 5; 24:16; אִישׁ בְּחֶטְאוֹ יוּמָֽתוּ׃ they shall be put to death, each because of his own sin (cf. Je 31:30; Ez 3:18, v. 18:17) 2 S 3:27; 14:7 (cf. Jon 1:14) Is 7:4 (‖ מִן) 28:7 (‖ מִן) 50:1; 53:5; 57:17; Je 51:46; ψ 5:8, 11; 6:8 (‖ מִן) 31:10; 32:3; 42:10; 90:9; 94:23. So in בַּאֲשֶׁר, בִּגְלַל (v. אֲשֶׁר, גָּלָל), & (sometimes) in בִּדְבַר at, through, the word of.… of the material with which a work is wrought, both absol. עָשָׁה בַזָּהָב to work with gold Ex 31:4; 1 K 7:14; and to make a thing with (in our idiom, of) gold Ex 38:8; Ez 7:20; 1 K 15:22 (בָּנָה). Without a verb Lv 13:52; 2 Ch 9:18. with for although, in spite of (cf. Germ. bei alle dem): Lv 26:27; Nu 14:11 בְּכֹל הָאֹתוֹת in spite of all the signs that I have wrought, Dt 1:32; Is 47:9; ψ 27:3; especially in the phrase בְּכָל־זֹאת for all this Is 5:25; 9:11, 16, 20; 10:4; ψ 78:32 al. (Cf. in Arabic بِمَا Qor 9:25.) of a standard of measurement or computation, with, by: Ex 12:4 בְּמִכְסַת נְפָשֹׁת by the computation of souls; Lv 5:25 al. בְּעֶרְכְּךָ by thy reckoning; Ez 4:10; בְּמִסְפָּר by number Dt 25:2 +; בְּמִסְפַּר … by the number of … Lv 25:15, 50; Nu 1:2, etc.; בְּשֶׁקֶל הַקֹּדֶשׁ Ex 30:13 al. (in P); בָּאַמָּה often (v. אַמָּה); Dt 3:11 בְּאַמַּת אִישׁ by the cubit of a man; 2 S 14:26 בְּאֶבֶן הַמֶּלֶךְ. Of a model, Gn 1:26 בְּצַלְמֵנוּ in our image, 5:1, 3; Ex 25:40; בְּדֶרֶךְ with the way (= in the manner) of … Is 10:24, 26; Am 4:10. בְּ is used also with certain classes of verbs, though the explanation of its use may be sometimes doubtful: viz. with verbs of taking refuge, trusting, relying, as הֶאֱמִין, בָּטַח, חָסָה, נִשְׁעַן. with verbs of ruling, governing, restraining, as מָלַךְ, מָשַׁל, נָגַשׂ, עָצַר, רָדָה, שָׁלַט. with verbs of rejoicing, feeling pleasure or satisfaction, etc., as גִּיל, עָלַץ, שׂוּשׂ, שָׂמֵחַ, חָפֵץ, רָצָה, שָׂבַע (but with this verb מִן is more common). [Prob. a case of III. 5.] with verbs expressive of sensible perception, to denote the pleasurable or attentive exercise of the faculty concerned, as שָׁמַע בְּ to listen to, הִבִּיט בְּ, רָאָה, חָזָה, to look upon, הֵרִיחַ בְּ to smell at (see these words). occasionally also with verbs of speaking, thinking, mentioning, knowing, to denote the object of the action, as דִּבֶּר בְּ to speak about Dt 6:7; ψ 87:3 al. (v. sub דִּבֶּר); הִלֵּל בְּ, הָגָה, שִׂיחַ ψ 44:9; 63:7; 69:13; יָדַע Je 38:24; זָכַר once Je 3:16, הִזְכִּיר often; ψ 71:6 בְּךָ תְהִלָּתִי of thee is my praise. Followed by an inf. c., בְּ forms a periphrasis for the gerund, though in English it is commonly to be rendered by a verb and conj., viz.:— as a temporal conj., as Gn 2:4 בְּהִבָּֽרְאָם in their being created = when they were created, 4:8 בִּהְיוֹתָם in their being (= when they were) in the field; and constantly. Sometimes it has in appearance the force of after that, as Gn 33:18 Ex 3:12; 13:17; but as a rule this is really due to the action denoted by the inf. being treated as extending over a period within which the action of the principal verb takes place: so especially in the phrase בְּצֵאת יִשְׂרָאֵל מִמִּצְרַיִם, even of events at the close of the 40 years, Dt 4:45; 23:5 Jos 5:4; the whole period being treated as that in which Egypt was left (comp. 2 K 2:1, where the time included is future). Cases, however, occur in which this explanation will hardly apply, as Dt 27:4, 12. as a causal conj. (cf. above iii. 5), as Gn 19:16 בְּחֶמְלַת י׳ עָלָיו through J.’s having compassion upon him, Ex 16:7; 33:16; 34:29 Dt 1:27 בְּשִׂנְאַת י׳ אֹתָנוּ through J.’s hating us, etc. (9:28 similarly מִן), 1 K 18:18 (= in that ye have …) Ez 9:8; 43:8; 44:7 2 Ch 28:6. as a concessive conj., when = though: Is 1:15 ψ 46:3 בְּהָמִיר אָ֑רֶץ though the earth do change. —Ex 10:12 בָּאַרְבֶּה can only be rendered ‘with the locusts,’ the locusts being conceived as implicit in Moses’ uplifted hand: but prob. לָאַרְבֶּה should be read. Thrice in late Heb. בְּ is used peculiarly: 1 Ch 7:23 for with misfortune was it in his house (בְּרָעָה chosen for the purpose of explaining בְּרִיעָה); 9:33 עֲלֵיהֶם בַּמְּלָאכָה it devolved upon them with the work; Ezr 3:3 for with terror (was it) upon them from the peoples of the countries (the sentence without a verb as often in Chr.: DrIntr. 504 f.): cf. 8:34. Comp. Ew§ 295 f.

---

### יִבְחֲנוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: בָּחַן  

vb. examine, try (cf. Aramaic בְּחַן, Syriac ܒܰܚܶܢ, try, examine; Arabic محن i. viiii. test, cf. WSG 65)— Pf. sf. בְּחָנַ֫נִי Jb 23:10, בָּחַנְתָּ ψ 17:3 + 7 times; Impf. יִבְחָ֑ן ψ 11:5 + 5 times; Imv. sf. בְּחָנֵנִי ψ 26:2 + 2 times; Inf. בְּחֹן Zc 13:9; Pt. בֹּחֵן Je 11:20 + 5 times (‖ צרף, נסה);— examine, scrutinize, try ψ 11:5; 139:23 Jb 7:18; עַפְעַפָּיו יִבְחֲנוּ בְנֵי אָדָם His eyelids try the children of men (search them through and through) ψ 11:4. prove, test, try. with the metaphor of gold Jb 23:10; וּבְחַנְתִּים כִּבְחֹן אֶת־הַזָּהָב and I will try them as one tries gold Zc 13:9. without metaphor, of God testing persons ψ 26:2; 66:10; 81:8 Je 9:6; ways Je 6:27 (prophet); the לֵב ψ 17:3 Je 12:3 Pr 17:3 1 Ch 29:17; reins Je 17:10; heart and reins ψ 7:10 Je 11:20 (= 20:12). of man testing or tempting God ψ 95:9 Mal 3:10, 15. the ear trying words Jb 12:11 = 34:3. Impf. יִבָּחֵן Jb 34:36 Gn 42:15, 16, to be tried, proved. בֹּחַן Ez 21:18 the trial has been made (𝔊 𝔖 𝔙 Ges MV Ew Ke, but noun בֹּ֫חַן MT Symm Haev; read בְּחֵן with grace, favour, Hi Co).

### חָמָס
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: חָמָס  

n.m. ψ 7:17 violence, wrong—abs. ח׳ Gn 6:11 + 44 times; cstr. חֲמַס Ju 9:24 + 7 times; sf. חֲמָסִי Gn 16:5; Je 51:35 (in both = wrong done to me); חֲמָסוֹ ψ 7:17; pl. חֲמָסִים 2 S 22:49 + 3 times;—violence specif. of physical violence Ju 9:24; 2 S 22:3 (not ‖ ψ 18:3), Ob10, Hb 1:9; Je 51:35 (of Chaldeans), Hb 2:8, 17(×2) Jo 4:19 ψ 72:14; but also wrong, incl. injurious language, harsh treatment, etc. Gn 16:5 (J, of wrong done to Sarah by Hagar), Jb 19:7 Mal 2:16; in gen. of rude wickedness of men, their noisy, wild, ruthlessness Mi 6:12 Hb 1:2; Zp 1:9 Pr 10:6, 11; 13:2; 26:6 + Ez 7:11 (si vera l., v. Co), ‖ שֹׁד Am 3:10; Hb 1:3; Je 6:7; 20:8; Ez 45:9; Is 60:18, ‖ רִיב ψ 55:10, ‖ עָמָל 7:17, ‖ גַּאֲוָה 73:6, אֹהֵב ח׳ 11:5 (‖ רָשָׁע), denied, of servant of י׳ Is 53:9, ח׳ בארץ Je 51:46, cf. Gn 6:11, 13 (P), Ez 8:17; 12:19; מָֽלְאוּ מַחֲשַׁכֵּי־אֶרֶץ נְאוֹת ח׳ ψ 74:20, הָעִיר מָֽלְאָה ח׳ Ez 7:23; 28:16, שֶׁבֶת ח׳ Am 6:3 is (prob.) enthronement of violence; כְּלֵי ח׳ Gn 49:5 (poem) instruments, weapons, of violence;—other phrases are: ח׳ בְּיָדַיִם Jon 3:8; 1 Ch 12:17, cf. Jb 16:17, ח׳ יְדֵיכֶם ψ 58:3 and פֹּעַל ח׳ בְּכַפֵּיהֶם Is 59:6; עֵד ח׳ i.e. a witness that promotes violence and wrong Ex 23:1 (JE), Dt 19:16, עֵדֵי ח׳ ψ 35:11; עֵדֵי שֶׁקֶר וִיפֵחַ חָמָס ψ 27:12; שׂנאת ח׳ ψ 25:19 = hatred characterized by violence; אִישׁ חָמָס = violent man ψ 18:49 (2 S 22:49 has the later א׳ חמסים, cf. infr.) 140:12 Pr 3:31; 16:29; אישׁ חמסים (later) ψ 140:2, 5; 2 S 22:49; יֵין חֲמָסִים Pr 4:17 i.e. wine gained by violence (‖ לֶחֶם רֶשַׁע).

### מְנָת כּוֹסָֽם

(Biblical Aramaic and 𝔗 מַנִּי);—Pf. מִנָּה Dn 1:10, 11; מִנּוּ Jb 7:3; Impf. וַיְמַן Jon 2:1 + 4 times; Imv. מַן ψ 61:8; appoint, ordain, food Dn 1:10, v 5 (sq. לְ), officer v 11 (sq. על); nights of weariness (לֵילוֹת עָמָל) Jb 7:3 (subj. indef.; sq. לְ); in story of Jonah, obj. fish Jon 2:1, ricinus 4:6, worm v 7, wind v 8 (all subj. י׳); obj. חֶסֶד וֶאֱמֶת ψ 61:8 (subj. י׳). ### צְדָקוֹת
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: צְדָקָה  

n.f. righteousness;—abs. צ׳ Gn 15:6 + 81 times; cstr. צִדְקַת Dt 33:21 + 5 times; sf. צִדְקָתִי Gn 30:33 +, etc.; pl. צְדָקוֹת Is 33:15 + 3 times; cstr. צִדְקוֹת Ju 5:11 +; sf. צִדְקֹתֶיךָ Dn 9:16, etc.;— righteousness, in government: of judge, ruler, king: ‖ משׁפט Is 5:7; 9:6; Am 5:7; 6:12; משׁפט וצ׳ 2 S 8:15, עשׂה D. executed justice and righteousness = 1 Ch 18:14, cf. 1 K 10:9 = 2 Ch 9:8, Je 22:3, 15; 33:15 Ez 45:9; בּצ׳ יִכּוֹן כִּסֵּא Pr 16:12, cf. Is 54:14; עֲבֹדַת הצ׳, מַעֲשֵׂה הצ׳ Is 32:17, cf. v 16; נֹגְשַׂיִךְ צ׳ Is 60:17. of law, ‖ משׁפטים, צִדְקַת י׳ Dt 33:21. of Davidic king, Messiah, ‖ משׁפט, ψ 72:1, 3; Is 9:6; Je 23:5; 33:15. God’s attribute as sovereign ψ 36:7; 71:19; in government, עשׂה משׁפט וצ׳ 99:4; Je 9:23; administering justice Jb 37:23; punishment Is 1:27; 5:16; 10:22; 28:17; Dn 9:7; vindication of his people Mi 7:9. righteousness, in a case or cause, בצדקתי החזקתי Jb 27:6 on my righteousness I hold fast; מה ישׁ לי עוד צ׳ 2 S 19:29 what right have I yet? (cf. Ne 2:20); of God, הגיד צ׳ Is 57:12 (iron.); השׁיב צ׳ 1 S 26:23; Jb 33:26; כּצ׳, השׁיב 2 S 22:25, גמל v 21, נתן 1 K 8:32 = 2 Ch 6:23. righteousness = truthfulness, באמת ובצ׳ Is 48:1; Zc 8:8; in word Is 45:23; 63:1, oath Je 4:2. righteousness, as ethically right: Gn 30:33 (J) Dt 6:25; Is 33:5; 48:18; Ez 14:14, 20; Pr 10:2; 11:4, 5, 18, 19 + 17 times, + צִדְקַת (ה)צַדִּיק(ים) Is 5:23; Ez 18:20; 33:12; צ׳ ישׁרים Pr 11:6; אֹרַח צ׳ 8:20; 12:28; דֶּרֶךְ צ׳ 16:31; עשׂה צ׳ do righteousness ψ 106:3; Is 56:1; 58:2; Ez 18:22; עשׂה משׁפט וצ׳ Ez 18:5 + 6 times Ez; עשׂה צ׳ ומשׁפט Gn 18:19 (J) Pr 21:3; רדף צ׳ Pr 15:9; 21:21; הלך באמת ובצ׳ 1 K 3:6; חשׁב לוֹ (ל)צ׳ Gn 15:6 (JE) imputed to him (for) righteousness, ψ 106:31; צ׳ וָחֶסֶד Pr 21:21(×2) (del. 𝔊 AB Toy). righteousness as vindicated, justification, salvation, etc. (cf. צֶדֶק 6): of God, ‖ יֶשַׁע, תְּשׁוּעָה, יְשׁוּעָה Is 45:8; 46:13; 51:6 + 7 times Is 2; ‖ בְּרָכָה ψ 24:6; ‖ נַחֲלָה Is 54:17; ‖ חֶסֶד ψ 36:11; 103:17; שֶׁמֶשׁ צ׳ Mal 3:20 sun of righteousness (with healing); בִּצ׳, י׳ delivers, guides, exalts his people ψ 5:9; 31:2; 71:2; 89:17; 119:40; 143:1, 11; אַל־יָבֹאוּ בְּצִדְקָתֶ֑ךָ 69:28 (of wicked); as acc. after verbs of declaring, etc., his saving (delivering) righteousness 22:32; 40:11; 51:16; 71:15, 16, 24; 98:2; 145:7; also יִוָּדַע צ׳ 88:13; צ׳ עֹמֶדֶת לָעַד his righteousness endureth for ever 111:3 cf. 119:142. of people, = prosperity, ‖ הון, עשׁר Pr 8:18; מוֹרֶה לצ׳ early rain for prosperity Jo 2:23. pl. righteous acts: of God Ju 5:11(×2); 1 S 12:7; Mi 6:5; vindication of right ψ 103:6; redemptive Is 45:24; Dn 9:16. of man’s moral conduct Is 64:5; Je 51:10, also prob. Ez 3:20; 18:24; 33:13 (Kt sg.) ψ 11:7 (? gloss), Dn 9:18; as adv. acc., הֹלֵךְ צְדָקוֹת Is 33:15.
